    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="5b580863dd2248a7b084b31c2ad605d1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '5839fce8-2fee-4463-a01b-b84a3eef1338'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d58409206" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A cost-efficient full-cell manufacturing of sodium-ion batteries (SIB) is presented by using the DRYtraec® process to avoid toxic N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone solvent. Resulting dry-processed cathodes with lower binder content were compared to wet-processed analogue. Paired with aqueous-processed anodes, the resulting prototype pouch-cells show competitive performance, stability for 400 cycles, and a reduced environmental impact, highlighting a promising path toward commercial SIB.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3bee8e3a-d952-45df-b70f-9bc6ba9c9e2b/batt202400572-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3bee8e3a-d952-45df-b70f-9bc6ba9c9e2b/batt202400572-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a758b969-254f-49eb-b82b-e49f986d77d0/batt202400572-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3bee8e3a-d952-45df-b70f-9bc6ba9c9e2b/batt202400572-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d58409292" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Achieving commercial viability for more sustainable sodium-ion batteries (SIB) necessitates reducing the environmental impact of production, particularly originating from electrode drying and the use of toxic solvents like N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP). This study presents the dry-processing of commercial P2-type Na<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.50</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) via the DRYtraec® process, aiming to lower the binder content of 1 wt.% polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) and eliminating the need for electrode drying and NMP recovery. Assessments of electrode morphology and active material crystallinity were conducted to gauge the effects of mechanical stress during processing. The resulting cathodes, loaded at a commercially relevant 2.3–2.7 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> loading, were successfully paired with aqueous-processed hard carbon (HC) anodes, demonstrating stable performance in full-cells. Comparative analysis with entirely wet-processed electrodes revealed comparable capacity accessibility and comparable long-term stability. This showed the competitiveness of dry-processed cathodes. Finally, the integration of NMP-free, dry-processed cathodes and aqueous-processed anodes was scaled to the commercially relevant prototype pouch-cell. The cell demonstrates stable cycling for 400 cycles with an energy density of 102 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> as well as reduced processing costs and environmental footprint.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202400572-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202400572-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Decarbonizing the world economy is an essential future target in the 21<sup>st</sup> century requiring energy from renewable energy sources. However, the fluctuation of wind and solar power and the transformation to electrified mobility pose an increasing demand for energy storage technologies.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0001_R_d58409087e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - World Economy">1</a>-<a href="#batt202400572-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0003_R_d58409087e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Renewable Energy">3</a></sup></span> In addition to a high energy density, these storage technologies should be reliable, safe, environmentally friendly, and cost-efficient.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0004_R_d58409087e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Carbon Energy">4</a></sup></span> In recent years, lithium-ion batteries (LIB) become the dominant technology that fulfills a part of these requirements, namely a high energy density, sufficient safety, and cost-efficiency.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0002_R_d58409087e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0005_R_d58409087e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> However, their cost advantage is strongly related to the prices of the raw materials incorporated in the cell. As the demand for LIB increases, especially due to the growth of the electric vehicle (EV) market, raw material costs will increase accordingly.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0001_R_d58409087e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - World Economy">1</a></sup></span> Besides low abundant critical active materials based on nickel or cobalt that could be replaced by lithium iron phosphate (LFP) or sulfur,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0006_R_d58409087e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Energy Storage">6</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0007_R_d58409087e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Joule">7</a></sup></span> total global reserves are limited with respect to lithium to approximately 26 million tons (resources 98 million tons).<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0008_R_d58409087e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> Therefore, the lithium price will further increase as a result of the continuous growth of energy storage demand, except when replacing the charge carrier lithium with another metal, such as sodium.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0009_R_d58409087e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Technol. Forecast. Soc. Change">9</a></sup></span> In a sodium-ion battery (SIB) the charge is transferred between both electrodes by sodium ions.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0010_R_d58409087e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">10</a></sup></span> While the drawbacks of SIB are still lower energy densities and a more complex intercalation mechanism in the cathode,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0011_R_d58409087e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">11</a>-<a href="#batt202400572-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0013_R_d58409087e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat Sustain">13</a></sup></span> the interest in this cell system increased in recent years.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0014_R_d58409087e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Russ. J. Electrochem.">14</a>-<a href="#batt202400572-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0017_R_d58409087e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span> A major reason is the fact that the sodium prices are significantly lower than the lithium prices, as sodium abundance exceeds the one of lithium by three orders of magnitude.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0014_R_d58409087e620" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Russ. J. Electrochem.">14</a></sup></span> Additional cost-savings are related to the replacement of the copper anode current-collector by aluminium.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0018_R_d58409087e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">18</a></sup></span> Furthermore, it is estimated that SIB reveal advantages in terms of carbon- and water-footprint with respect to LIB or Li−S batteries.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0019_R_d58409087e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Cleaner Prod.">19</a></sup></span> At the current state, lower energy densities of SIB are outbalancing cost savings from sodium raw material price and current collector replacement, leading to a higher material and energy demand per kWh storage as well as higher production cost.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0018_R_d58409087e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">18</a></sup></span> Hence, the manufacturing process of cells becomes the major contributor to cost per kWh.</p>
         
         <p>To address these current limitations, the contribution of production and environmental footprint are faced by switching from solvent-assisted electrode coating to solvent-free coating as a promising opportunity.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0020_R_d58409087e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Next Energy">20</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0021_R_d58409087e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - IEEE Trans. Eng. Manage.">21</a></sup></span> Solvent-free coating does not require (toxic) solvents, such as N-methylpyrrolidone (NMP), in the electrode production chain which is part of the current state-of-the-art manufacturing for LIB as well as SIB.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0022_R_d58409087e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">22</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0023_R_d58409087e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Sci. China Mater.">23</a></sup></span> Thereby, the energy consumption of the electrode coating process could be reduced by 75–85 %,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0020_R_d58409087e641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Next Energy">20</a></sup></span> as no drying and solvent recovery steps are necessary. Since these production steps become redundant, the initial investment required for production plants is lower as well. Additionally, the dry coating step reduces the footprint of the plant by 15–30 %,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0020_R_d58409087e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Next Energy">20</a></sup></span> enabling a further reduction of the investment cost. An especially promising dry coating approach is the dry transfer electrode coating process or DRYtraec®, as it allows the facile and solvent-free fabrication of dry electrodes without handling delicate free-standing films. <sup>[20,24,25]</sup> Previously, it was demonstrated that this coating process is compatible with a wide variety of active materials, ranging from graphite over Li<sub>a</sub>Ni<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>Co<sub>z</sub>O<sub>2</sub> to next-gen materials such as sulfur, and even solid electrolytes by several groups.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0021_R_d58409087e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - IEEE Trans. Eng. Manage.">21</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0026_R_d58409087e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a>-<a href="#batt202400572-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0030_R_d58409087e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Res.">30</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In this work, we applied this cost-efficient coating technique to fabricate solvent-free cathodes for SIB with ultra-low binder content and reduced footprint, reported for the first time. Therefore, the active material, layered transition metal oxide Na<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.50</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode (NFM) was selected due to its high potential with respect to abundant precursors as well as a high specific capacity of up to 208 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0031_R_d58409087e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span> A characterization using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and X-ray diffraction (XRD) before and after DRYtraec® processing is performed to evaluate the impact of the coating routine on the active material. The as-prepared NFM cathodes as well as wet-based hard carbon anode (HC) are electrochemically evaluated using a carbonate-based electrolyte (ethylene/diethylene carbonate, EC/DEC with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>) at half-cell level and compared to a wet-processed analogue, afterwards. Finally, the combination of processed electrodes in full-cells is investigated at coin-cell and pouch-cell level including postmortem analysis.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202400572-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202400572-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Cathodes were fabricated using commercially available NFM material and 1 wt.% binder through the solvent-free DRYtraec<sup>®</sup> process and NMP-based wet-processing methods. HC anodes were prepared with an aqueous-processing approach. The resulting cathodes and anodes reveal areal capacities exceeding 2.3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> as characterized in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#batt202400572-tbl-0001">1</a>.
</p>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400572-sec-0003-title">2.1 Surface Morphology of Dry-Processed Layered Oxide Cathodes</h3>
            
            <p>NFM-based cathodes were characterized using SEM and focused ion beam SEM (FIB-SEM), as shown in Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0001">1</a>. The applied processing method affects the active material morphology, with pristine NFM-particles displaying intertwined plate-shaped lamellae (Figure S 1d–f), while the cathode surface particles appear flattened (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0001">1c</a>). The presence of PTFE-fibrils is visible in Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0001">1c</a>, connecting NFM agglomerates whereas carbon additives fill the spaces between particles, forming a good percolation network (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0001">1e</a>).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202400572-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/465dfc5f-b61f-49fa-8c36-56dcaf765ad1/batt202400572-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/465dfc5f-b61f-49fa-8c36-56dcaf765ad1/batt202400572-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ebc7ceae-ea21-4b42-84a7-cacdbec9fa0c/batt202400572-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/465dfc5f-b61f-49fa-8c36-56dcaf765ad1/batt202400572-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202400572-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202400572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>SEM micrographs (a–c) of NFM-based dry-film cathodes produced via DRYtraec® as well as the corresponding FIB-SEM micrographs (d–f) of the electrode cross-section.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The FIB-SEM cross-section of the dry-cathode in Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0001">1d</a> shows a gradient in densification, with higher compaction at the electrode surface and lower compaction in the bulk. A comparision between Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0001">1f</a> and Figure S 1e confirms that the NFM particle morphology within cathode bulk remains largely intact. This densification gradient suggests that NFM particles experience flattening primarily in the direct contact zone between the roller and the composite.</p>
            
            <p>This effect can be attributed to two factors. First, increaded heat impact at the roller during the lamination step might be linked to particle deformation as the powder film is pressed onto the current collector foil. Second, lamination introduces two distinct mechanical forces: a static force exerted by the roller carrying the powder film and a shear force applied by the second roller transporting the current collector. The interaction of these forces result in plastic deformation of particles, leading to cracking, flattening, or reorganization while maintaining the overall morphology. This behavior is comparable to the effects observed in electrode calendering.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0032_R_d58409087e944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>A high suitability of the DRYtraec® process was concluded from a promising film-formation (Figure S 2) and a retained particle shape inside the electrode bulk. However, a deformation of the NFM particles at the cathode surface requires the investigation of crystallinity and electrochemical performance as performed in the following sections.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400572-sec-0004-title">2.2 Influence of Processing on the Cathode Active Material Crystallinity</h3>
            
            <p>XRD analysis in reflective Bragg-Brentano geometry was performed on pristine NFM powder and as-prepared DRYtraec® cathodes to assess any adverse effects on the NFM crystalline structure. From the comparison of diffractograms, a material degradation would be detectable by emerging amorphous material contributions as well as changes in inter-layer spacing by water intercalation.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0033_R_d58409087e958" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Environ. Sci.">33</a></sup></span> Both investigated samples of Na<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.50</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exhibited P2-type layered structures with a space group of P6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> (Figure S 4).<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0034_R_d58409087e976" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Mater. Sci. Mater. Electron.">34</a></sup></span> Detailed NFM crystalline structure and lattice parameters were reported previously by Lan et al.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0034_R_d58409087e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Mater. Sci. Mater. Electron.">34</a></sup></span> and Chen et al..<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0035_R_d58409087e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>In Figure S 4, no amorphous contribution or chance in interlayer spacing were observed between pristine NFM and NFM within processed cathodes, suggesting that dry-processing under nitrogen atmosphere had no significant impact on the composition or crystal structure of the layered oxide. However, α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> signals at 41° and 68° within the pristine materials and the processed electrode, suggest potential contamination or unfavorable synthesis condition by the producer.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0036_R_d58409087e991" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">36</a></sup></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400572-sec-0005-title">2.3 Three-Electrode Cell Measurement at Half-Cell Level</h3>
            
            <p>Initial tests in half-cells are performed to assess specific capacities of dry and aqueous processed cathodes and to compare voltage curves. Processed HC and NFM electrodes were assembled as the working electrode in three-electrode half-cells, with a sodium metal counter electrode and sodium metal reference. This cell configuration was chosen to mitigate reported sodium overpotential.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0037_R_d58409087e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chemistry of Materials Chem. Mat.">37</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0038_R_d58409087e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">38</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>From Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0002">2a</a>, a comparable cathode charging capacity of 119 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> during the first formation cycle between 2.0–4.1 V highlights a good comparability between both electrodes. Moreover, no deviation in addressable capacity is noted from half-cell results, suggesting no active material damage or delamination during surface compaction during the DRYtraec<sup>®</sup> process as identified in section 2.1. The dQ/dV diagrams (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0002">2c</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0002">d</a>) allow a comparison of respective capacity contributions. In Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0002">2b</a>, cathodic capacity contributions within two regimes could be identified for both cathode types, namely 2.7–4.0 V and &gt;4.0 V. A identification of the capacity contributions to Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> at 2.1 V/2.0 V, Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> at 3.7 V/3.6 V and Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> as well as Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> for a voltage higher than 4.0 V was performed on basis of the reported P2-type NaNi<sub>0.15</sub>Fe<sub>0.20</sub>Mn<sub>0.65</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material by Yuan et al..<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0039_R_d58409087e1051" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Electrochim. Acta">39</a></sup></span> Notably, a higher structural stability is expected due to a broader capacity contribution at 2.7–3.4 V of the investigated Na<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.50</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in comparison to the reported NaNi<sub>0.15</sub>Fe<sub>0.20</sub>Mn<sub>0.65</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with a lower nickel content.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0039_R_d58409087e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Electrochim. Acta">39</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0040_R_d58409087e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span> A shift of voltage curve after the first cycle by 6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was related to the selected lower voltage limit of 2 V resulting in a partial sodiation of open vacancies of the P-type layered transition metal oxide.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0041_R_d58409087e1079" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Power Sources">41</a></sup></span> Latter is only present in half-cells against sodium counter-electrode and cannot be utilized in full-cells leading to the selection of first cycle charging capacity for full-cell balancing of the cathode.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202400572-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3fcd4ba6-7cba-418f-a35f-5a4a299ac5c0/batt202400572-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3fcd4ba6-7cba-418f-a35f-5a4a299ac5c0/batt202400572-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/705c2154-47e9-4fa4-b55d-b7af9eeac45d/batt202400572-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3fcd4ba6-7cba-418f-a35f-5a4a299ac5c0/batt202400572-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202400572-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202400572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Resulting voltage profiles from three electrode half-cell measurements of NFM<sub>Dry</sub> and NFM<sub>Slurry</sub> with corresponding differential voltage plot dQ/dV (a, c). The hard carbon anode voltage profile as well as the differential voltage plots (b, d). Dotted graph indicates 3<sup>rd</sup> cycle.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The HC<sub>Slurry</sub> anode (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0002">2c</a>) showed an initial discharging capacity (sodiation) of 323 mAh g<sup>− 1</sup> (ICE: 85.9 %) at 0.05 C, with continuous cycling resulting in a reversible capacity of 273 mA g<sup>− 1</sup> (CE: 98.8 %). A plateau at 0.7 V (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0002">2c</a>) can be attributed to the oxidation of FEC additive, used for stabilizing the sodium metal counter electrode, emphasized in the dQ/dV plot (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0002">2d</a>).<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0042_R_d58409087e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">42</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>First cycle discharging capacity of the HC anode was selected for full-cell balancing.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400572-sec-0006-title">2.4 Electrochemical Characterization at Full-Cell Level</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0007">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="batt202400572-sec-0007-title">2.4.1 Comparison on Coin-Cell Level</h4>
               
               <p>The Full-cell performance of NFM<sub>Dry</sub> vs. HC<sub>Slurry</sub> was compared by measuring NFM<sub>Slurry</sub> vs. HC<sub>Slurry</sub> for an N/P ratio of 1.0 in a voltage window of 4.1–1.5 V. A comparability to half-cell results was achieved by the selected N/P ratio with respect to the voltage shift in full-cells as shown in Figure S 7. The cells were cycled after an initial formation at 0.05 C from 0.1 C towards 3 C during an asymmetrical rate capability test. Latter was extended by continuous cycling until 124 cycles at 0.5 C as illustrated in Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0003">3</a> and three cycles of 0.1 C to compare the changes in voltage contribution.
</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="batt202400572-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1b41c2ea-21fe-4008-8740-23f1a5fc2d85/batt202400572-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/1b41c2ea-21fe-4008-8740-23f1a5fc2d85/batt202400572-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5db05e89-53fb-4006-9070-5311b124c939/batt202400572-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1b41c2ea-21fe-4008-8740-23f1a5fc2d85/batt202400572-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202400572-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202400572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                           <p>Discharge capacities of full-cells assembled from processed cathodes NFM<sub>Dry</sub> and NFM<sub>Slurry</sub> against HC<sub>Slurry</sub> (a) as well as voltage curves from the third cycle of each current rate during the rate capability test. (b). Impact of rate-capability test as well as long-term cycling are illustrated in dQ/dV plots of respective cycles for NFM<sub>Dry</sub> vs. HC<sub>Slurry</sub> (c) and NFM<sub>Slurry</sub> vs. HC<sub>Slurry</sub> (d) at 0.1 C.</p></div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               
               <p>Specific discharge capacities of 100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (ICE: 83.4 %, C<sub>s, charge</sub>: 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and 98 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (ICE: 86.1 %, C<sub>s, charge</sub>: 114 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) were obtained for NFM<sub>Dry</sub> vs. HC<sub>Slurry</sub> and NFM<sub>Slurry</sub> vs. HC<sub>Slurry</sub> configuration, respectively (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0003">3a</a>). A good agreement with the half-cell measurements in three-electrode setup is indicated by a comparable charge capacity. The lower ICE of SIB full-cells (83–86 %) compared to half-cells (~90 %) is widely reported and was related to trapped capacity within the HC anode in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>/HC full-cells.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0043_R_d58409087e1232" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">43</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0044_R_d58409087e1232" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">44</a></sup></span></p>
               
               <p>The continuous cycling at 0.1 C as well as the voltage curves of the rate performance test until 2 C, depicted in Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0003">3b</a>, reveal slightly higher values for the full-cell capacity in comparison to wet-processed cathodes showing comparable active material utilization. In detail 89 % (0.5 C) and 81 % (2 C) of the initial discharge capacity (0.05 C) were accessible. A further increase in C-rate towards 3 C revealed a difference in rate capability between 50 % for NFM<sub>Dry</sub> and 78 % for NFM<sub>Slurry</sub> indicating a reduced active material utilization. These observations might relate to a difference in compaction during electrode preparation which is evident by the density 1.8 g cm<sup>−3</sup> and 1.3 g cm<sup>−3</sup> for dry-processed and wet-processed cathodes. A difference in ionic transport resistance is therefore expected based on the SEM images and difference in rate capability. Hence, higher currents reveal a limitation by ionic diffusion within the dry-processed cathode as already described for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0032_R_d58409087e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0045_R_d58409087e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Power Sources">45</a></sup></span></p>
               
               <p>During subsequent cycling until 124<sup>th</sup> cycle, a 9 % decay in capacity from 87 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for NFM<sub>Dry</sub> and 84 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for NFM<sub>Slurry</sub> was found during 100 cycles at 0.5 C. A final CE value for the 124<sup>th</sup> cycle was found to be 99.89 % and 99.97 % for NFM<sub>Dry</sub> and NFM<sub>Slurry</sub>, respectively. Hence, continuous cycling results in a comparable capacity decay whereas a lowered CE of NFM<sub>Dry</sub> hints towards capacity losses related to a constant leak of charges. A reformation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) should be considered due to the increased roughness of electrode surface found from cathodes extracted by post mortem analysis shown in Figure S 7. From dQ/dV plots in Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0003">3d</a>, a comparable decay of each capacity contribution was measured. The comparison of the 3<sup>rd</sup> and 127<sup>th</sup> cycle shows a constant reduction of peak intensities for charge and discharge as well as a shifting of peaks towards higher voltage for charge. An in-depth DVA of full-cell degradation is out of the scope of this work, but a trend for increasing cell resistance was identified according to literature.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0032_R_d58409087e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0046_R_d58409087e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Prog. Energy Combust. Sci.">46</a></sup></span></p>
               
               <p>This increase in cell overpotentials agrees with a fast decay in capacity contribution over initial cycles for the contribution of Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> as well as Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3</sup> close to the upper voltage limit at 4.1 V identified in section 3.3. An increase of resistance due to sodium metal plating was excluded from further consideration due to the lack of evidence from post-mortem analysis depicted in Figure S 7b.</p>
               
               <p>A comparable capacity decay of 9 % (0.5 C) during continuous cycling as well as a decrease of each capacity contribution in the dQ/dV plots showed a comparable degradation of full-cell capacity on coin-cell level, irrespective of the type of cathode. The difference in rate capability depending on the electrode processing was identified and was explained by the process-related impact of electrode densification leading to a difference in ionic transport resistance.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0008">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="batt202400572-sec-0008-title">2.4.2 Investigation in Pouch-Cells</h4>
               
               <p>Prototype single-layer pouch-cells were evaluated for rate capability, long-term cycle life as well and energy density calculations on stack-level. Therefore, dry-processed cathodes were combined with wet-processed hard carbon anodes. An increase in N/P ratio towards 1.1 is recommended to avoid risk of sodium metal plating due to a higher electrode surface area that could be prone to inhomogeneity in areal capacity. Initial discharge capacities of measured cells are 106 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (ICE: 85.2 %, C<sub>s, charge</sub>: 124 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) for 2.7 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and 101 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (ICE: 84.6 %, C<sub>s, charge</sub>: 119 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) for 2.4 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0004">4a</a>). These values agree with a charge capacity of 119 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> determined from three-electrode half-cell measurements of section 3.3 and full-cells on coin-cell level in section 2.4.1. With respect to cycle life, the upper voltage limit was decreased from 4.1 V towards 4.0 V to account for the increase in N/P ratio compared to section 3.4.1. An asymmetric rate test was conducted to verify the accessible capacity for discharge rates from 0.1 C towards 2 C. An emerging deviation can be found with increasing C-rate resulting in slightly higher capacity values for the lower loaded electrodes. Again a limitation of ionic diffusion can be expected as observed for the comparison of rate capability between high density dry-processed cathodes and wet-processed cathodes on coin-cell level in section 2.4.1.
</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="batt202400572-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/776a9ae9-a286-4087-a561-5a472cd18548/batt202400572-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/776a9ae9-a286-4087-a561-5a472cd18548/batt202400572-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/38b87ef7-0aa3-40a1-bc15-c3a1cad9773d/batt202400572-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/776a9ae9-a286-4087-a561-5a472cd18548/batt202400572-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202400572-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202400572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                           <p>Discharge capacities as well as CE for single layer pouch-cells based on NFM<sub>Dry</sub> vs. HC<sub>Slurry</sub> for different loadings (a) as well as long-term cycling results up to 400 cycles (b).</p></div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               
               <p>From long-term cycling to 400 cycles, a capacity decay of 19.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> or 26 % (CE<sub>400th</sub>: 99.75 %) and 15.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> or 23 % (CE<sub>400th</sub>: 99.89 %) were derived for a cathode loading of 2.7 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and 2.4 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively. A detailed distribution of CE values from Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0004">4b</a>, reveals a more stable CE of &gt;99.9 % for electrodes with lower areal loading whereas higher loadings seem to cause fluctuating CE values between 99.7–99.9 % resulting in an accelerated degradation of the cell.</p>
               
               <p>A maximum specific energy of 102 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and an energy density of 139 Wh L<sup>−1</sup> were calculated on stack-level for initial cycles with an average voltage of 3.3 V and 70.7 mAh capacity for cells with a cathode loading of 2.7 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. From literature a specific energy of up to 165 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> was reported based on layered oxide cathode and HC anode.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0011_R_d58409087e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">11</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0047_R_d58409087e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Electrochim. Acta">47</a></sup></span> With respect to further energy density optimization, which was not in the scope of this work, lower electrolyte amounts need to be tested. For the reported cells, a mass contribution of 49 % from the electrolyte was estimated. Calculations on the electrode porosity by Equation S 1 and Equation S 2 showed an excess of electrolyte of 263 % based on 1 ml (14 μL mAh<sup>−1</sup>) electrolyte with respect to the available electrode pore volume. An excess of 150 % (0.57 ml | 8 μL mAh<sup>− 1</sup>) of the electrode porosity was selected for possible application from LIB<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0048_R_d58409087e1407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - MRS Advances">48</a></sup></span> which would lead to a specific energy of 130 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (184 Wh L<sup>−1</sup>). Therefore, the optimization of electrode densities, electrolyte amount<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0048_R_d58409087e1414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - MRS Advances">48</a></sup></span> and loading<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0049_R_d58409087e1418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">49</a></sup></span> towards high energy multi-layer pouch-cells are planned.</p>
               
               <p>With respect to cell degradation, post-mortem analysis on anode and cathode were conducted using SEM measurements on FIB cuts and energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) after 400 cycles. The comparison of cathodes before and after cycling (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0005">5</a>) reveals a slight increased inter-particular spacing within the electrode bulk. In contrast, the interface between electrode coating and primed current collector in Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0005">5</a> c,d does not indicate the appearance of particle-delamination. To take into account, the lower adhesion strength of the derived dry-processed cathodes from Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#batt202400572-tbl-0001">1</a> and higher reported elasticity of PTFE compared to PVDF, the swelling of the electrodes should be expected.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0029_R_d58409087e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Technol.">29</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0050_R_d58409087e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Ind. Chem. Mater.">50</a></sup></span>
                  </p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="batt202400572-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9fa59a7d-2e20-48a2-8d30-73b32dd6fb0d/batt202400572-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/9fa59a7d-2e20-48a2-8d30-73b32dd6fb0d/batt202400572-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3a3439db-5434-4c05-a78d-1b1e7907fa82/batt202400572-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9fa59a7d-2e20-48a2-8d30-73b32dd6fb0d/batt202400572-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202400572-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202400572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                           <p>SEM micrograph of dry-processed cathode in pristine form and extracted after 400 cycles. (a, b) comparison of electrode bulk and (c, d) current collector interface.</p></div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               
               <p>From Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0006">6b</a>, the FIB cut of the anode reveals a 50 μm thick sodium layer on the surface as well as a coating of active material particle identified as SEI within the electrode bulk (Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0006">6c</a>). The EDX graphs in Figure <a href="#batt202400572-fig-0006">6d</a>–<a href="#batt202400572-fig-0006">h</a> show a particle coating out of sodium oxide as well as cathode metal species such as Mn, Fe and Ni. The presence of sodium oxide is caused by the contact to atmosphere during transport.
</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="batt202400572-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/04724fc9-ac34-44a6-95b6-5e3a9da34a41/batt202400572-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/04724fc9-ac34-44a6-95b6-5e3a9da34a41/batt202400572-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/60528ce6-c30b-44bd-a2f5-52ce77dc8f56/batt202400572-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/04724fc9-ac34-44a6-95b6-5e3a9da34a41/batt202400572-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202400572-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202400572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                           <p>SEM micrograph of anode extracted after 400 cycles with partial sodium metal plating (a) and FIB cutting of area with strongest metal plating (b) as well as enlarged cross section marked by orange frame (c). EDX results of the enlarged cross section for sodium, oxygen, manganese, iron and nickel (c–h).</p></div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               
               <p>As described by Che et al., the dissolution and migration of NFM cathode material species in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) and ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC) towards the anode might cause sodium metal plating.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0051_R_d58409087e1501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Power Sources">51</a></sup></span> The presence of sodium metal plating on the anode including cathode active material species confirms a comparable dissolution of the investigated NFM in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DEC. Herein, the need for electrolyte optimization with respect to new solvents and additives (i. e.: FEC, VC, 1–3 PS)<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0051_R_d58409087e1506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Power Sources">51</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0052_R_d58409087e1506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">52</a></sup></span> becomes evident.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202400572-sec-0009">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202400572-sec-0009-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>This study demonstrates the solvent-free processing of commercial NFM to cathodes with 1 wt.% PTFE binder which is reported for the first time, paving the way to a cost-efficient and NMP-free scalable production technique like the DRYtraec® process. Using XRD and SEM characterization, the preservation of active material crystal structure and its morphology were shown throughout the processing. The produced cathode contains a remarkably low binder content of only 1 wt.% and an active material content of 96 wt.%, thus reducing the amount of passive materials. A further reduction of the production footprint could be realized by the combination with an aqueous-processed HC anode yielding full-cells on coin-cell and pouch-cell level utilizing a NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt in EC/DEC electrolyte. The comparison to state-of-the-art wet-processed cathodes based on the critical NMP solvent reveal a high performance compared to the solvent-free processed cathode on coin-cell level. A comparable rate performance up to 2 C (81 % compared to 0.05 C) towards a fully wet-processed full-cell reference was demonstrated. Higher C-rates show a reduced rate capability that was caused by the compaction of electrode films without additional calendering step, indicating the need for further investigation of the relation between electrode compaction and cell performance by a calendering study.</p>
         
         <p>At the same time, the degradation of full-cells based on dry-processed cathodes was found to be comparable to the wet-processed reference. However, first cycle capacity losses and a comparable steady degradation for both electrodes revealed limitations.</p>
         
         <p>Finally, single-layered pouch-cells with commercially relevant electrode loadings of 2.7 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and 2.4 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> were manufactured and demonstrated to reach up to 400 cycles until 80 % end-of-life and an energy density of 102 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Based on post-mortem analyses using SEM-EDX, the degradation was linked to local plating of sodium metal on the anode. Latter was linked to the presence of cathode active material species within the anode solid electrolyte interface (SEI). These findings reveal a dissolution and migration of transition metal ions for NFM and thereby the need for improved electrolyte compositions.</p>
         
         <p>Overall, these first prototypes demonstrate a successful transition from wet-processing towards dry-processing of SIB cathodes via DRYtraec® process. However, the production of optimized SIB multi-layer pouch-cells requires the study of electrode density and fabrication of double-sided electrodes as well as new electrolyte formulation based on this proof-of-concept. In this way, the cost for production was decreased whereas the production footprint was reduced. Further research and development with respect to electrode density and electrolyte composition are desirable to promote a higher sustainability and performance of this technology with respect to energy density.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202400572-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202400572-sec-0010-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0011">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400572-sec-0011-title"> Cathode Fabrication</h3>
            
            <p>The layered transition metal oxide-based dry-processed cathodes were prepared using the patented DRYtraec® process.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400572-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0024_R_d58409087e1549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a>, <a href="#batt202400572-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400572-bib-0025_R_d58409087e1549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25">25</a></sup></span> The dry-cathode composite was yielded by blending NFM (BN−P2B, BTR New Material Group Co., Ltd.), conductive carbon (Super C65, Imerys S.A.), multi-walled carbon nanotubes (Nanocyl 7000, Nanocyl S.A.), and high molecular weight emulsion polymerized PTFE in a ratio of 96/2/1/1 with a blade mill (M20 Universal mill; IKA-Werke GmbH &amp; Co. KG). The composite preparation and cathode fabrication, with the respective calender system, were conducted in a nitrogen-filled glovebox (dew point: −55 °C, Fraunhofer IWS). The electrode films were formed by applying shearing modulus by differential roll speed with a production capacity of 2 m min<sup>−1</sup>. A process temperature of 110 °C and a hydraulic pressure of 240 bar were applied. The generated film was laminated onto a primer aluminium current collector foil (18 μm Al including 1 μm). The target loadings were 19.8 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and 24.3 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> which equates to an areal capacity of 2.3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and 2.7 mAh cm<sup>2</sup><sub>,</sub> respectively. For comparison, a wet-processed cathode was produced in a composition of 96/2/1/1 wt.% utilizing the same active material and conductive additives. The PTFE binder was replaced by a state-of-the-art PVDF (PVDF 7200, Kureha Corp.) dissolved in NMP (99.8 %, Roth). An oscillating mill (MM-400, Retsch) was selected for mixing, with the use of five steel balls (10 mm) and a frequency of 10 Hz for 10 min for powder mixing. Once the binder-solvent solution was added, another mixing at 25 Hz for 10 min was applied. The resulting suspension was cast onto a primer aluminum current collector foil (18 μm Al including 1 μm primer) using a doctor blade (ZUA 2000, Zehntner Testing Instruments) and a speed of 50 mm s<sup>−1</sup> (Model 510, Ericsen). The cathodes were dried at 110 °C under nitrogen for 15 min in an oven (Thermo Fisher Scientific) and then transferred to vacuum.</p>
            
            <p>Adhesion strength was determined for mechanical characterization using a uniaxial material testing machine (2.5 kN ZwickiLine, Zwick GmbH &amp; Co. KG) with bond tester sample holder (Zwick GmbH &amp; Co. KG). Therefore five measurements of 15 mm electrode discs were conducted with 20 Hz data acquisition rate under force control. A 12 mm adhesive tape disc was pressed on the sample with 70 N compression force with 4 mm min<sup>−1</sup> for 10 s dwell time and a pull-off velocity of 4 mm/min.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0012">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400572-sec-0012-title"> Anode Fabrication</h3>
            
            <p>In the case of the wet-processed reference anode, the as-received Kuranode Type 2 (9 μm) hard carbon (Kuraray Co., Ltd.) was combined with a modified SBR binder (SSBR 100, Targray) and a CNF conductive additive (Pyrograf III®, Pyrograf Products Inc.) in a ratio of 90/5/5 wt.%. The powders were mixed by oscillatory milling (MM-400, Retsch) using a 50 ml crucible with five steel balls (d: 10 mm) and a frequency of 10 Hz for 10 min. Subsequently, a further step at a frequency of 25 Hz for 10 min was used after the addition of the solvent. The suspension was then cast onto a primer aluminum current collector foil (18 μm Al including 1 μm) using a doctor blade (ZUA 2000, Zehntner Testing Instruments) at a speed of 50 mm s<sup>−1</sup> (Model 510, Ericsen). For electrode drying, an oven (Thermo Fisher Scientific) under atmosphere at 110 °C was employed. The active material loading of hard carbon anodes fabricated was 7.8 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, while their areal capacity was 2.6 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. For comparison, a higher loading of 9.2 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and 3.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> was prepared as well.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0013">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400572-sec-0013-title"> Surface Morphology</h3>
            
            <p>The surface of the produced electrodes and during post-mortem analysis were examined via SEM and EDX. All samples were affixed to the sample holder with an adhesive carbon. Air-sensitive samples were transported to a FIB/SEM instrument (JIB 4610 JOEL Ltd.) using a transfer chamber developed in-house. The FIB cross-sections were created by bombarding the sample surface with gallium ions. An acceleration voltage of 5 kV and a working distance of 14 mm were employed. The recording angle was set at 53°. Additionally, the surface morphology of the electrodes was characterized using SEM. These measurements were conducted using a JSM 7800 (JEOL Ltd) instrument, with an acceleration voltage of 5 kV. The working distance was 4.5 mm.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0014">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400572-sec-0014-title"> X-ray Diffraction</h3>
            
            <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns for the NFM samples were recorded on an Empyrean-3 Alpha 1 system X-Ray diffractometer with a Cu K<sub>α1</sub> anode (λ=0.154056 nm) operating at 40 kV and 40 mA in Bragg-Brentano reflection geometry. The diffraction patterns were collected at 25 °C and over an angular range of 5 ° to 70 °. The applied reflex free silicon wafer was covered with Kapton tape to protect the sample from fast degradation.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0015">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400572-sec-0015-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0016">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="batt202400572-sec-0016-title"> Half-Cell Tests</h4>
               
               <p>Prior to cell assembly, electrode discs with a diameter of 18 mm were subjected to vacuum drying at 110 °C for 12 h. A three-electrode setup (ECC-Ref, EL-CELL) was assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (MBraun) under conditions of &lt;0.1 ppm O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O. A glass fiber separator (thickness: 1500 μm; diameter: 18 mm), a sodium composite chip counter-electrode (thickness: 420 μm Na and 30 μm Al; diameter: 15.6 mm; AOT battery) and a sodium metal rod reference punched from sodium metal cubes (99.9 % Na; Sigma Aldrich) were employed in the assembly of cells. Prior to sealing, 350 μL electrolyte was added. A commercially available mixture of ethylene and diethylene carbonate (EC/DEC 4/6 v:v) with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> was employed as the electrolyte (E-Lyte Innovation GmbH). Prior to cell assembly, 5 wt.% FEC (99.9 %, Solvionic) was added. Following assembly, the cells were allowed to rest for a period of 12 h at 25 °C. The electrochemical characterization was conducted at a constant temperature of 25±2 °C using a Biologic VMP-3 system (Biologic). Galvanostatic cycling was conducted with a constant current rate of 0.05 C within a voltage window from 0 V to 1.5 V for anodes (1 C=330 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and from 2.0 V to 4.1 V for cathodes (1 C=115 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) in relation to Na/Na<sup>+</sup> using potential control by the working electrode against reference (Ewe-Ref). A high resolution of dQ/dV graphs was ensured by setting the voltage registration criterion to 1 mV. Each measurement was conducted using a double determination.</p>
               
               <p>For electrochemical impedance spectroscopy, the same cell-setup was applied. The measurement was conducted under constant temperature of 25±2 °C using a Biologic VMP-3 system (Biologic). For the formation, two cycles at constant current rate of 0.05 C within a voltage window 2.0 V to 4.1 V (1 C=115 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) in relation to Na/Na<sup>+</sup> using potential control by the working electrode against reference (Ewe-Ref) was applied. After formation the cells were cycled at 0.1 C for 25 cycles with a check-up cycle after 5 cycles. During the check-up cycle a PEIS configuration under cell voltage potential control (Ewe-Ece) was used. A range from 1 MHz to 1 Hz was applied with an amplitude of 20 mV and 10 points per decade in logarithmic spacing with 2 averaged measures per frequency. The cathode potential was hold at 2.0 V before measurement corresponding to 100 % SOC.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0017">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="batt202400572-sec-0017-title"> Full-Cell Tests</h4>
               
               <p>The electrodes for full-cell measurement were first punched out to a diameter of 16 mm and 15 mm for the HC anode and NFM cathode, respectively. Prior to cell assembly, the electrodes were dried at 110 °C under vacuum for 12 h. NFM vs. HC full-cells were constructed as CR2016 coin-cells in an argon-filled glovebox (MBraun, conditions: &lt;0.1 ppm O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O) using a 25 μm PP separator and 100 μL of the electrolyte. The electrolyte utilized was the aforementioned ethylene and diethylene carbonate (EC/DEC 4/6 v:v) with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> and without FEC. Prior to testing, the cells were allowed to rest for 12 h at 25 °C. Electrochemical tests were conducted at 25±2 °C with a BaSyTec cell testing system (BaSyTec GmbH, Germany) in a voltage range between 1.5 V and 4.1 V. The formation of the cells was achieved through three cycles of 0.05 C CCCV (0.025 C) and 0.05 C CC discharge. Subsequently, ten additional cycles were conducted at 0.1 C. Following the thirteenth cycle, an asymmetric rate capability test was performed with a constant charge rate of 0.3 C CCCV (0.05 C) and CC steps with discharge rates of 0.5 C, 1 C, 2 C and 3 C for three cycles, each. For the long-term cycling, a charging rate of 0.3 C CCCV (0.05 C) and 0.5 C for discharge was selected until 80 cycles. A triple determination was employed.</p>
               
               <p>Pouch-cells were assembled using a single-sided anode, single-sided cathode, 25 μm PP separator and 1 mL (13.0 μL mAh<sup>−1</sup> and 14.8 μL mAh<sup>−1</sup>) electrolyte (1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DEC 4/6 v:v + 2 wt.% 1,3-PS). The cell manufacturing process was conducted in an argon-filled glovebox (MBraun, conditions: &lt;0.1 ppm O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O). An external uniaxial pressure of 0.076 MPa was applied during the cycling process and was controlled by a pneumatic regulator (Fraunhofer IWS). The cell tests were conducted on a BaSyTec cell testing system (BaSyTec GmbH, Germany). In order to accomplish initial formation, a current of 0.05 C CCCV (0.025 C) for charge and 0.05 C CC for discharge was applied in a voltage window of 1.5–4.1 V for four cycles. Following the formation, the upper voltage limit was lowered to 4.0 V. An asymmetric rate capability test was then performed with a constant charging current of 0.3 C CCCV (0.05 C), while the CC discharge rates were 0.1 C, 0.5 C, 1 C and 2 C, respectively, for three cycles. During long-term cycling, a current rate of 0.3 C CCCV (0.05 C) for charging and 0.5 C for discharging the cell was selected. The cells were subjected to continuous cycling until they reached the end-of-life criterion of 80 % of their initial charge capacity. All measured specific capacities were related to the mass of active material.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

         <div class="accordion article-accordion">
            <h2><a href="#" class="accordion__control" role="button" aria-controls="open-research-content" aria-expanded="false"><span class="section__title" id="open-research-section">Open Research</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a></h2>
            <div class="accordion__content" role="region" aria-labelledby="open-research-section" id="open-research-content">
               <h1></h1>
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400572-sec-0000">
                  
                  <h1>Data Availability Statement</h1>
                  
                  <p>The data that support the findings of this study are available from the corresponding author upon reasonable request.</p>
